  The hard parts (be ready for these)

  1. GLB scaling will fight you. Your ~80 reused models have wildly different
  native scales and some have weird empty bbox space. The fit-to-footprint logic
   is right in principle but in practice you'll get a sofa that's mouse-sized or
   a lamp that fills the room. Solution: a per-asset / per-category override 
  table ‚ÄĒ { "sofa_3seat.glb": { scaleMul: 1.1 }, ... } ‚ÄĒ tuned by
  trial-and-error with Chrome screenshots. Budget real time for this; it's the
  single fiddliest thing left.
  2. The collaborator only needs to be bulletproof on the demo phrases. Don't
  try to make Kimi-K2 handle everything. Test the exact 4‚Äď5 things you'll say in
   the demo ("make the sofa bigger", "warmer", "add a study desk", and one
  savings prompt), harden the intent extraction + /apply round-trip for those,
  and you're good. A vague "make it nicer" can give a fluffy answer ‚ÄĒ that's
  fine, it shows it has a voice.
  3. Live 3D demos glitch ‚ÄĒ WebGL context loss, slow GLB loads, layout jumps on
  first render. Record a full backup video, rehearse the live demo 3+ times,
  memorise the intake values you'll type. The senior move isn't a flawless live
  demo; it's having the backup and a smooth recovery.
  4. The fake catalog ‚ÄĒ don't hide it. When you show variants in the demo: "84
  base 3D models; the ~3,600 SKUs are parametric size/material/finish variants ‚ÄĒ
   a real launch is a data partnership with Pepperfry, Urban Ladder, etc."
  Owning it reads as product maturity.

  The internship story

  A working, interactive 3D home-design flow + an opinionated AI collaborator +
  a carpenter-ready quotation with a Hindi section, demoed cleanly in 15 minutes
   ‚ÄĒ that's a genuinely strong intern project. And the narrative you can tell
  sells you harder than the pixels: "I architected a brain/face split with a
  typed contract; I caught a coordinate-convention bug that made layouts feel
  random and fixed the whole system to footprint-centre; when the 3D wasn't
  where it needed to be I made an honest call to lock the foundation and wrote a
   handover doc; here's what I'd partner-source vs. build for v1." That's how a
  senior engineer talks. Tell it that way.

‚úĽ Crunched for 1m 6s

‚ĚĮ can you for a second act like the ceo or the one with this idea and think     
  about it                                                                      

‚óŹ Okay. Let me be the person whose idea this is for a minute. Not the engineer
  worrying about GLB scaling ‚ÄĒ the founder, the one who got annoyed enough at
  something to start this.

  ---
  Here's what I actually believe, and why I started Nirmit.

  I'm not building a 3D design tool. There are a dozen of those and they're all
  the same: a blank floor plan, a furniture catalog, drag the boxes around, good
   luck. They're built by people who think the problem is "I can't visualize my
  room." That's not the problem. The problem is a woman in Thane standing in an
  empty flat with ‚āĻ3 lakhs she's terrified of spending wrong, a Pinterest board
  full of rooms that aren't hers, a Livspace quote for ‚āĻ7L that made her feel
  like prey, and a carpenter ‚ÄĒ Suresh ‚ÄĒ sitting there saying "madam aap batao 
  kahan kya chahiye." She has the money. She doesn't have the confidence. She
  can't picture it, she doesn't trust her own taste, and the two options on the
  table either bankrupt her or require her to be the designer. Nirmit is a 
  confidence machine. The job is: take her from overwhelmed to ready-to-act, in
  ninety minutes, for free, without ever making her feel sold to. Everything
  else ‚ÄĒ the renders, the AI, the catalog ‚ÄĒ is plumbing.

  And here's the thing the big players structurally cannot do: Suresh isn't
  going anywhere. He's cheap, he's trusted, he's already in her WhatsApp, and he
   can build ‚ÄĒ he just can't design. Livspace's whole business is replacing him
  with a locked contractor and a ‚āĻ5L floor. That's why they're expensive. We
  don't replace Suresh. We arm him. We hand the customer a quotation ‚ÄĒ real
  product names, real prices, a clean floor plan with a North arrow, a section
  in Hindi written the way a contractor talks ‚ÄĒ and she walks it over to the guy
   she already trusts. That document is the product. It travels. It goes to her
  husband, to Suresh, to Suresh's friend who "knows about these things," to her
  cousin in Pune. If it's good, it sells itself, and we never spent a rupee on
  her acquisition.

  So if you ask me, the founder, "is this a business?" ‚ÄĒ honestly: the tool is 
  the wedge; the carpenter network is the business. The tool catches her at the
  exact moment of highest intent ‚ÄĒ when she's deciding. The money is in the
  transaction layer underneath: verified Sureshes, a small fee for the intro,
  eventually maybe escrow and a quality guarantee. That's a marketplace with
  network effects. Nirmit-the-SaaS is a thin layer that gets crushed;
  Nirmit-the-thing-that-connects-Priya-to-a-vetted-carpenter-with-a-plan-he-can-
  actually-build is defensible. The cultural specificity is the moat ‚ÄĒ Vastu as
  spatial logic, the guest's entrance view, the steel almirah that's staying,
  the pooja space facing east, the grandmother who needs a chair with arms. A
  Bangalore team gets that. A San Francisco team building a generic tool never
  will. That's not a feature list, that's why we win.

  Now ‚ÄĒ what would I tell my team if I were running this? You built it in the 
  wrong order. You poured weeks into the planner ‚ÄĒ the drag-to-move, the intent
  menu, the 2D/3D toggle ‚ÄĒ and the reveal is still rough and the quotation PDF
  needs a Devanagari font. That's backwards. The reveal is v1. The quotation is
  v1. The planner is v2 ‚ÄĒ it's a retention feature, the thing that keeps her
  coming back to tweak; you don't need it to prove the thesis. The thesis is:
  she answers four questions, we show her her flat three ways, she feels 
  something, she screenshots it, and then we hand her a quote her carpenter can 
  actually use. If those two moments ‚ÄĒ the reveal and the artifact ‚ÄĒ land, I
  have a company. If they don't, no amount of drag-to-move fixes it. The
  riskiest assumptions in this whole thing are exactly the two you
  under-invested in: (1) can the system produce rooms that feel designed, not
  like a catalog dump, and (2) will a real carpenter look at our PDF and nod
  instead of laugh. Everything that matters lives in those two questions.

  The only metric I care about for v1 is the one the vision doc already named:
  did she send the screenshot. Unprompted. No share button. That's it. Not DAU,
  not retention curves ‚ÄĒ did a woman in Thane look at the render of her own
  living room and feel recognition and reach for her phone. Ten of those, in ten
   cities, different budgets, different vibes ‚ÄĒ that's traction. Before that
  happens reliably, it's a promising prototype. After, it's a killer product.
  The entire job is perfecting the things that produce that one moment.

  ---
  So ‚ÄĒ translating that back for you, since you asked: stop trying to demo the 
  19-week vision in 15 minutes. Demo the thesis, looking real. Intake ‚Üí reveal
  (the three views, the "we put the mandir in the northeast because Vastu
  matters to your family," the cost story that says "35% of what Livspace quoted
   you") ‚Üí the quotation Suresh can build from. Then show the planner and the AI
   collaborator as "and it keeps going ‚ÄĒ you can mix these, you can talk to it,
  you can tweak everything" ‚ÄĒ show it for two minutes, don't stake the demo on
  it being flawless. That's not scope-cutting. That's telling the true story of
  what Nirmit is ‚ÄĒ a wedge into the moment of decision and the transaction
  underneath it ‚ÄĒ instead of a tour of every screen. It's the right product call
   and, conveniently, the right demo call, because it puts your polish hours
  where the bet actually lives.

  If I were pitching this to your interviewers, that's the version I'd show. And
   that's the version I'd build next.

‚óŹ Understood. The deliverable is the whole thing ‚ÄĒ full flow, 15-minute demo, no

  "The entire project" is not an open-ended quest anymore. It's a finite punch 
  list. The hard, scary part ‚ÄĒ the architecture, the brain/face split, the
  contract, the solver, the wiring of every screen ‚ÄĒ that's done. What's left
  isn't "build the rest." It's "make what's wired look decent, feel solid, and
  not break in front of the panel." That's maybe 30‚Äď50 discrete tasks. A long
  list, but a list ‚ÄĒ enumerable, prioritizable, grindable. You don't need genius
   from here; you need a checklist and discipline. Here's the checklist.

  P0 ‚ÄĒ demo-killers (if these are broken, a chunk of your 15 minutes is dead)

  1. Wire GROQ_API_KEY ‚ÄĒ Groq's free tier is genuinely free. Without it the AI
  collaborator is a stub and ~3 min of your demo is hollow. ~15 min of work.
  2. Cold start clean ‚ÄĒ npm install && npm run dev + uvicorn work from a fresh
  clone, no first-open crash, "Your Home" handles the empty state.
  3. GLB scaling sanity ‚ÄĒ a mouse-sized sofa or a room-filling lamp reads as
  "couldn't finish." Add a per-asset / per-category scale-override table, tuned
  by trial-and-error with Chrome screenshots. This is the fiddliest item; budget
   for it.
  4. Reveal renders cleanly ‚ÄĒ no black screen, no WebGL error, no camera
  clipping through the back wall.
  5. Planner select ‚Üí Move ‚Üí drag ‚Üí place works without the camera spinning out
  (the fix is in the code ‚ÄĒ verify it in Chrome).
  6. /apply round-trip for the exact demo chat phrases ("make the sofa bigger",
  "warmer", "add a study desk") produces a visible change. Harden these specific
   paths.
  7. PDF downloads and opens, Hindi section renders (register a Devanagari TTF
  in boq/pdf.py ‚ÄĒ currently it'll be tofu boxes).

  P1 ‚ÄĒ "this feels janky" (won't break the demo, but undercuts it)

  - Reveal: ceremony timing (fade ‚Üí hold beat ‚Üí reveal), the three camera views
  framing well, swipe between visions smooth, the reasoning + cost panels
  readable.
  - Planner: rotate/duplicate working, finishing mode actually changing the 3D
  (paint/flooring/lighting warmth), the cost pulse on changes, the camera-preset
   bar, the "Apply this change?" proposal card.
  - Intake: copy on the 4 questions, vibe cards looking decent, slide
  transitions, the budget slider.
  - 3D fidelity: atmosphere props not floating/clipping, contact shadows under
  furniture, wall-fade smooth.
  - States: generating-screen narrative timing, API-down ‚Üí graceful error,
  empty/loading states.
  - Page transitions between stages ‚ÄĒ re-add Framer Motion (small dep, makes a
  demo feel finished).

  P2 ‚ÄĒ do only if time allows

  Real vibe photos instead of SVG; better GLB models/materials; mixing-feature
  polish; multi-room beyond living/bedroom; mobile responsive. None of these are
   required for a complete demo of the flow.

  P3 ‚ÄĒ demo logistics (not code, but mandatory)

  - Write the 15-minute script ‚ÄĒ narration + clicks, minute by minute. Build
  every session toward "make minute X land."
  - Rehearse it 3+ times. Memorise the intake values you'll type.
  - Record a backup video of the full flow ‚ÄĒ 3D demos glitch live; you want a
  recovery.
  - Tighten the README; you already have SESSION_HANDOVER.md; add an honest

   a key; stylized 3D).
  - Be ready to narrate the engineering: why the brain/face split, why
  mm-everywhere, why you refactored to footprint-centre coords, what you'd
  data-partner vs. build for v1.

  - Rough budget: P0 ‚Čą 3‚Äď4 sessions, P1 ‚Čą 5‚Äď7, P3 ‚Čą 1‚Äď2 + rehearsal. So ~10‚Äď15 
  Code sessions + interleaved Chrome QA over ~2‚Äď3 weeks. That's a normal
  internship-project timeline. It's finishable.

  The reassurance you actually need

  An intern who delivers the entire flow ‚ÄĒ working, interactive, not breaking, 
  demoed cleanly in 15 minutes ‚ÄĒ with a coherent story about the architecture
  and the product bet, has cleared the bar. Big tech is not asking you for
  production Livspace or photoreal renders. They're asking: can this person 
  build a real, full, customer-facing thing and reason about it like an

   a punch list away. "Photoreal and perfect" is not the assignment, so don't
  let the gap between what's-built and the-vision make you think you're further
